B0012HA29O|5.0|There's the stupid debate on audio quality on a record or on a CD. The debate is as sterile as the knowledge of the recording and printing process everyone has on these recordings. I've heard music meant to be burned to CD as well as compressed to MP3s cut to records and it sounds as awful on record as it sounds on every other format and that's because of the process in question (Jamiroquai's Dynamite is the best example). If you like music like this record you should ALWAYS try to get a original copy of the original pressing. Why? Because of that process: The two track master tape was taken to the cutting stage where a very talented and savvy 'cutter' did the final adjustments to the playback of that master tape at the moment of fitting everything on a groove (cutting the record, literally). That process is called \\"the cut\\" and is that crucial process which gave the sound its character; its punch. If you get a \\"re-masterized\\" or just digitized version of the same material, the process was redone by another much less talented guy replacing the cutting process, or maybe it was skipped all along and the tape was just transferred to a DAT tape, then to an MP3 without the care and love of the guys that were hoping to have a hit on that unknown master tape back in the day. and THAT'S what makes an LP sound better than CD or MP3 (besides the compression and yada yada). The only way to keep the original character of a record is to digitize it to a CD and clean the crackle a bit, so don't ever hesitate on getting the original pressing of a record and enjoying it the way it should be. Then take that and burn a CD from there.

B004J4WQ46|2.0|Sorry, couldn't resist. Corny, I know.    I wanted to love this record, because not only did I love The Strokes' first two albums, but I was one of the few fans who much enjoyed First Impressions. That Angles leaves me ambivalent might be even worse than if I hated it. Divisiveness is a hallmark of worthwhile \\"art\\". Never mind whether or not the Strokes are relevant; good songs are always relevant, no matter what kind of bells and whistles are trendy. Unfortunately, there are only three or four good numbers here and the rest is tepid, stitched-together filler.     What amazes me is that this record follows a five year hiatus. The band in no way sound refreshed. In fact, they sound hungover and depleted from a year-long world tour. The band itself in interviews claim that they can do better and that even they know this effort is a mixed bag. Well, why the hell release it then? Contractual obligation? Money? Certainly not out of passion. Bands have forged whole discographies in 5 years. Take a look at The Fall. Mark E. Smith is almost 54 years old, but the Strokes sound twice his age, weary and jaded before their time. There's always been an element of dispassion in The Strokes' work, but the actual *songs* never sounded that way.    The music? \\"Under Cover of Darkness,\\" \\"Gratisfaction,\\" and \\"Taken for a Fool\\" are stand outs. Nothing as good as any of the cuts from their first two records, but sturdy enough to seem like hooky out takes from that era. And I might be in the minority, but I also enjoyed the Car-sy muted chord progression and cheesy synths on \\"Two Kinds of Happiness\\"---that is, before the song erupts into an ill-fitting storm of drums and guitar.    The rest? There are glimmers here and there, but they seem to float out of the ether and disappear before blossoming into full-fledged songs.     I don't enjoy leaving negative reviews of bands whose prior work I admire and enjoy, but I think it would take a tremendous amount of charity to give this album anymore than 2.5 stars. The Strokes might very well release another record, if only to fulfill contractual obligations, but The Strokes, essentially, are no more. This is an odd-and-sods grab bag of ideas that the band members created independently, a Frankenstein record. It's clear that some of the members want to move on, and they should. I think they'll find much more inspiration when pursuing their separate ways, as Julian Cassablancas did with his much more impressive solo record.

159420229X|5.0|After stumbling on to the competitive memory circuit, the author embarks on an unlikely quest to become the memory champ in the next U.S. competition. This book chronicles his journey and provides a fascinating account about the history and science of memory along the way.<br /><br />I started reading this book knowing very little about the art of memory other than a basic definition of mnemonics. The author describes many of the techniques that he used to increase his memorization skills, including:<br /><br />Memory Palaces: Places you create in your mind to store visual memories<br /><br />The \\"Major System\\" for memorizing numbers phonetically<br /><br />POA Systems for memorizing numbers and/or cards: Person-Object-Action sequences for individual numbers (more interesting than it sounds)<br /><br />This information was brand new to me. As Foer is introduced to these techniques, you can feel his enthusiasm for learning new ways to use his brain. The art of memorization is all but lost in our current society of externalized data storage, but it does not have to be that way. The techniques that this memoir introduces will start you down the same path if you like; either way, it's a great read.<br /><br />In fact, once I started reading this book I could not put it down. The entire memoir is well written and keeps the reader's interest page after page. Though this is certainly not a how-to-book, it is a great starting point to learn more about memory principles.<br /><br />The author includes really interesting discussions about memory and creativity throughout. Though they may seem to be opposites at first glance, the author argues, compellingly, that they go hand-in-hand. My favorite chapters were:<br /><br />The Memory Palace: Details the history of this visual/spatial memory technique and point the reader in the right direction if they want to learn it.<br /><br />The End of Remembering: This chapter chronicles the reasons behind why we don't commit things to memory anymore. This account was very interesting, especially for the implications in scripture memory. I am guilty of reading widely at the cost of reading deeply.<br /><br />The Talented Tenth: This short chapter discusses some of the implications of memory and our school systems. Very interesting account of how memorization came to be disdained in the school system and the consequential effects.<br /><br />The Little Rain Man In All Of Us: Talks about the processing power of our brains and what savants can teach us. This chapter includes very interesting interviews with the man who inspired Rain Man and Daniel Tremmet, the subject of the documentary Brainman.<br /><br />The whole book is a really great read and is highly recommended. The only thing missing is a further recommended reading list. It will entertain you and possibly inspire you to practice the art of memory for yourself; it has inspired me to commit to memory many of the things that I have forgotten many times over.ications in scripture memory. It might have been a rough proccess to write and record and there might have been some in fighting, Lead Singer Casablancas even joked recently that he was jealous that Jack White was now going solo. The one thing that has not sufferded is the music as The Strokes sound as vital now than when they first burst onto the scene ten years ago, lets hope we don't have to wait another 5 years for an album as \\"Angles\\" is a great album!

B0010X745K|4.0|While this movie has a much maligned ending (which I won't spoil for anyone who hasn't viewed this yet...) ,what people don't seem to understand is that movies based on books must make certain concessions and even outright retooling to make them either viable or interesting to a larger audience who buy or sit through a film based solely (for the most part at least) on previews or word of mouth. Being a HUGE Stephen King fan and Constant Reader I can empathize with fellow fan's bewildered or even apoplectic reaction to changes made to their favorite book or novella once it finally gets to the big or small screen, hell even Steve himself has thrown a King-sized (i'm sorry...) hissy fit about the treatment of \\"The Shining\\" by Kubrick, which, if you know the reason, is understandable as well as a little relatable. Anyway, the point is that it is moronic to overlook a good piece of cinema just because \\"Hey,Norton isn't supposed to be BLACK!!!\\" or \\"Hey, Ollie Weeks doesn't wear GLASSES!!!\\", like I said...certain concessions. Yes, if you have read the short story the ending and alot of the ancillary characters are different, lines are redistributed, events are shuffled subtly, but the bones of the story remain and make for a good watch. As stated before, yes the ending is different, RADICALLY DIFFERENT, but as pole-axed as I was upon my first viewing, I ultimately realized that in some ways it was really no less valid when viewed as an adaptation and retelling, and much better suited to the medium and the wider audience than the original. My point is, from Stephen King geek to others out there that might have been turned off by friends or reviewers, pick this movie up and accept it for what it is and not what it was SUPPOSED TO BE. If you're like me then at least it's one to cross off the list and take it's place on the shelf between \\"The Langoliers\\" and \\"The Night Flier\\".

B004L49K20|5.0|`All You Need Is Now' is Duran Duran's 13th studio album and put quite simply, is easily their best album since `Rio' back in 1982!    Although I have been a fan of this band for some 28 years, I certainly don't live in a Duran Duran bubble, where my obsession clouds my judgement; because I am the first admit that they have made some pretty poor albums over the years. Some fans of the band do have selective hearing and believe that everything Duran Duran creates is music gold, which is simply not true. However, here we have been presented with an album which is 100% pure solid gold! and its sheer quality and brilliance lies in the song writing, which is really saying something for a band who have been around for 30 years. Also, you have to applaud the genius that is Mark Ronson, he has breathed new life into a Duran Duran which had lost its direction slightly with previous album `Red Carpet Massacre', although I personally thought that the album was not bad at all, but the R'n'B sound of that album was not what the fans wanted and it bombed. Mark Ronson came in and reminded the band of their history and completely revived them and their sound. On the whole the album sounds essentially like Duran Duran circa 1981-84, but there are also elements that remind you of the `Notorious' period and even into the 90's with `The Wedding Album'. This album could have so easily ended up sounding dated but it doesn't, as Ronson's production manages to keep it sounding fresh and current.    The albums title track is probably more 90's Duran Duran than 80's, but it's an absolute gem and features a soaring chorus that simply sets the tone for the rest of the album.    `Blame The Machines' is a song inspired by a true story of a man who took his Sat Nav directions too literally and ended up with him driving the wrong way down a one way street! Great pace and drive in this track and I love the guitars and the synths that runs right through it. There is an excellent part in the middle where ITV newsreader Nina Hossain brilliantly provides the dominating voice of the controlling Sat Nav!    `Being Followed' sounds like it could have been on `Rio'. A song essentially about paranoia, it has a totally infectious chorus which compliments the verses wonderfully. Dom Brown's guitars shine again, this time sounding like The Cure at times.    According to many fans the lush `Leave A Light On' sounds like `Save A Prayer', although for me it sounds more similar to the somewhat forgotten great Duran Duran track `Out Of My Mind'. The song is a beautifully constructed, with a laid back, late summers evening vibe, stunning!.    `Safe' is disco funk and features Ana Matronic from the Scissor Sisters with a great rap/vocal which can only be likened to Debbie Harry's on Blondie's `Rapture'. By no means the best song on the album, but by not bad either, its just that some fans have stated that it maybe sounds more like the Scissor Sisters featuring Duran Duran, and they could be right.    `Girl Panic!' is proper classic Duran Duran! It's like it 1981 all over again. Shouts `Hit' at you very loudly and at their peak would have been a massive hit for sure. Someone recently said that it sounded like all of Duran Duran's best singles mashed into one song and that is not far off the mark. Genius!    `The Man Who Stole A Leopard' is my favourite song on the album. What do we have in here?, well there are elements of `Tel Aviv' and `The Chauffeur' and a full orchestra (Conducted by Owen Pallet - Arcade Fire) which just adds to the song and adds a dark atmosphere to the song, words really can't do this track justice. Six minutes of Duran Duran at their very very best, just sit back and immerse yourself into what is set to become an all time Duran Duran classic, it really is quite glorious (Featuring backing vocals by Kelis and News Report by Nina Hossain).    `Other People's Lives' is yet another great moment. A fantastic chorus and also reminds me a little bit of `Great Escape' era Blur!    `Mediterranea' is a shimmery stunner. A mid paced song about getting away from it all, this really could become the new `Ordinary World'.    `Too Bad You're So Beautiful' goes back to the `Rio' era Duran Duran. If you ever loved `Hold Back The Rain and `Last Chance On The Stairway' then you will adore this.    `Runway Runaway' and they just keep on coming, another cracker this, but more of a `Seven & The Ragged Tiger' vibe here. Effortlessly catchy. My only gripe here is that it's over too quickly!    `Before The Rain' is a superb way to end the show, so to speak! Dark and brooding, it slowly builds and builds, right up to the intense marching band drums at the end. That works in favor of the dozen tunes that rest uneasily together on the vocalist's second LP \\"I Remember Me.\\"<br /><br />A singer who is not also a songwriter depends on the talent of others to create material prior to the point his or her own work is required. That presents a double-edged sword for Hudson here - the tunes are primarily banal and unmemorable, with sloppy, uninteresting choruses and unimaginative, lifeless production, yet they also make her energy and investment as a singer - that is to say, her raw talent - especially evident.<br /><br />The material is not world-class, but the singer is.<br /><br />Hudson skews toward the bombastic, at many times radiating the aura of an over-rehearsed actor, but elsewhere she gifts the songs with pulse, heart and soul, even though they rarely deserve it. She has a voice capable of stripping the varnish off a foot locker and she is unafraid to use it.<br /><br />She sounds best on the 70s soul/disco mash-up \\"Everybody Needs Love,\\" with its syllable-bursting energy and simple, effective chorus. \\"Angel\\" is also above average with soldier march beats. It makes great use of Hudson's range and expressiveness. So do \\"Why Is It So Hard?\\" and \\"Don't Look Down,\\" the latter of which bounces along with uncontainable energy and hard-won, rallying optimism. She shines, unsurprisingly, on the seminal \\"Feeling Good.\\"<br /><br />Songs with anti-intellectual, dumbed-down titles such as \\"No One Gonna Love You\\" and \\"Where You At\\" are named in a way that reflects their earthbound, uninsightful content. They insult the listener in how they pander to what Hudson's core audiences (supposedly) already know and expect. Those two tunes, as well as \\"I Got This,\\" are mildly pleasing in spite of their lack of exciting, interesting choruses and poor lyrics only because of Hudson's gusto and salesmanship. That applies also to Diane Warren's \\"Still Here,\\" a slice of musical high fructose corn syrup.<br /><br />Many names line the production credits yet the sound is largely singular throughout - icy, calculated and absolutely devoid of warmth and passion. The sounds are fresh out of a toolbox, with hardly a musician in sight. Hudson cannot help but be disadvantaged by this. She would have been more at home in the era when Luther Vandross was recording his most important records.<br /><br />\\"I Remember Me\\" is a flawed album. It is not unenjoyable, and it serves as a nice affirmation of Hudson's vocal prowess, but it will not leave a lasting impression on the popular culture or individual listener.uctose corn syrup. B004J4WQ46|4.0|The Strokes ride into town at 120 miles per hour with pistols drawn on \\"Angles.\\" The songs sway and shimmer with unbridled ebullience, consummate musicianship and fast-moving, lightening-sharp (but not calculated) precision that do not slip away until the album's short, sweet (34-minute) playing time has elapsed.<br /><br />The results are simply inspiring, and certainly ear-pleasing.<br /><br />Nick Valensi's inspired guitar-playing accentuates the mercurial, idiosyncratic vocals of lead singer Julian Casablancas so uncannily well that the reality that Casablancas recorded his vocals separately is difficult to accept - even a bit disappointing. But the sounds that emanate from high-octane cuts such as \\"Metabolism\\" and the atmospheric, breathy \\"Two Kinds of Happiness,\\" both helped along by Fabrizio Moretti's precise, emphatic drumming, speak for themselves.<br /><br />The Strokes are a little less Peter Gabriel, a little more Elton John this time around. That is to say, while the depth and content of previous records may not be in full evidence here, the choruses are often riper and stronger. \\"Angles\\" is ideal for blaring at full blast on summer drives through shore towns, not for Zach Braff to mine for material for his next quasi-indie soundtrack.<br /><br />Complaints that the band are at unsure artistic crossroads are not without credibility. \\"Angles\\" is the sound of a band taking all of its most fail-safe, commercially savvy elements and overstuffing ten muscular slices of guitar pop with them. If the results are not a sign of progression, they are neither a sign of stagnation or lack of attention - the end product is clearly one carefully honed and considered. The swashbuckling, hook-heavy \\"Under the Cover of Darkness\\" and the equally effusive \\"Taken for a Fool\\" are proof enough of that.<br /><br />A joyous, intelligent album of delightful musical confection should not be overthought or undersold, and that is precisely what \\"Angles\\" is. The dedication of these musicians to top-quality songcraft positively overflows. It may not win The Strokes an onslaught of converts - perhaps their soon forthcoming fifth record will come closer to the full breadth of their artistic promise - but it is certain to please the listeners for whom it was intended.ively overflows. The second is that the experiences Kvothe lived through didn't really show us much of a change or growth in his character. B004J4WQ46|3.0|It really does pain to write these words particularly after reading the huge claims of the recent BBC review that \\"Angles\\" the new record by the Strokes \\"might actually be their very best record yet\\". Thus hopes were raised and a genuine feeling of excitement was generated at the prospect. However we are back in musical Groundhog Day and even guitarist Nick Valensi has since admitted that \\"I feel like we have a better album in us\\". Frankly on this evidence you sense that may this might just be wishful thinking and sadly for those who have invested hours of prayer trying to persuade the great music God in the sky to smile again on J Casablancas and co a sense of disappointment will follow.<br /><br />It's not that \\"Angles\\" is a bad album, it's pretty good in some parts and a couple of the songs are top drawer. At times when the band kick into that great Strokes groove on the classy single \\"Under cover of darkness\\" and the brilliant power pop of \\"Two kinds of happiness\\" and \\"Taken for a fool\\" the great times come flooding back and you think ....yes! Perhaps the early weight of expectation on this New York band was always a mighty millstone to carry but it did seem possible at the time of \\"Is this it\\" that the ghosts of Television, Talking Heads and mutter it quietly the Velvet Underground were being resurrected and rejuvenated. From the amazing debut onwards to every subsequent release you wanted them to succeed with every ounce of your soul. The narrative behind the album however reveals a band reluctantly coming together after five years with Casablancas agreeing to a democratic input from all band members reflecting all angles (although recording his vocals separately). But the result of all this collaboration inevitably leads to a lack of coherence. In this sense the comment in Slant magazine is painful but probably true that \\"most of the time the album sounds like a divorce settlement being pieced together one painstaking line at a time\\".<br /><br />Thus we have \\"Games\\" sounding like a bad Human League song, the silly opener \\"Machu Pichu\\" starts off with cod reggae like a latter day \\"Dreadlock Holiday\\" and then somehow mutates into the Thompson Twins \\"We are detective\\". The bottom is truly scrapped with the tuneless dour synth pop of \\"Your so right\\"; while \\"Call me back\\" shows no sign whatsoever of ever working out what type of song it wants to be. Its true that \\"Gratisfaction\\" has a nice pop sensibility but it sounds like it was lifted from a Thin Lizzy song. Things finish strongly however with two great songs the powerful \\"Metabolism\\" and the albums highlight the genuinely lovely \\"Life is simple in the moonlight'.<br /><br />\\"Angles\\" then is a decent Strokes album, which is the biggest criticism you can level at it. Ultimately it's the sound of a band that used to be cool and edgy struggling to be relevant and wondering what to do next. Can even the most uncritical Strokes fan say with hand on heart that this album is even on par for example with the very underrated \\"Room on fire\\"? Indeed I think it has great deal of continuity with \\"First Impressions\\". Listening to \\"Angles\\" is like witnessing a once great team pulling in different directions and looking for someone to take charge on the field of play. Sorry to rain on the parade here since I truly wanted this to be brilliant and to signal that the Strokes have a glorious future. But it is with heavy regret that I have to say that it doesn't appear to achieve either of those aims.\\"Games\\" sounding like a bad Human League song, the silly opener \\"Machu Pichu\\" starts off with cod reggae like a latter day \\"Dreadlock Holiday\\" and then somehow mutates into the Thompson Twins \\"We are detective\\". The bottom is truly scrapped with the tuneless dour synth pop of \\"Your so right\\"; while \\"Call me back\\" shows no sign whatsoever of ever working out what type of song it wants to be. Its true that \\"Gratisfaction\\" has a nice pop sensibility but it sounds like it was lifted from a Thin Lizzy song. Things finish strongly however with two great songs the powerful \\"Metabolism\\" and the albums highlight the genuinely lovely \\"Life is simple in the moonlight'.<br /><br />\\"Angles\\" then is a decent Strokes album, which is the biggest criticism you can level at it. Ultimately it's the sound of a band that used to be cool and edgy struggling to be relevant and wondering what to do next. Can even the most uncritical Strokes fan say with hand on heart that this album is even on par for example with the very underrated \\"Room on fire\\"? Indeed I think it has great deal of continuity with \\"First Impressions\\". Listening to \\"Angles\\" is like witnessing a once great team pulling in different directions and looking for someone to take charge on the field of play. Sorry to rain on the parade here since I truly wanted this to be brilliant and to signal that the Strokes have a glorious future. But it is with heavy regret that I have to say that it doesn't appear to achieve either of those aims.
4770031246|4.0|\\"The Just Bento Cookbook: Everyday Lunches to Go\\" is not your typical cookbook.  It is sectioned off mostly into meals, which at first takes some adjusting to navigate efficiently.  While I get the intent of putting full meal examples together (with great alternatives for both main and side item variations and options), I feel a bit jumbled when trying to find a recipe for the cabbage I want to use, or just looking for a small side to add to an almost complete meal.   The flip side to that is great examples of how to organize full meals, including morning scheduled time charts to efficiently put them together in 15 minutes before rushing off to work!    The book contains a lot of bento recipes, tips, and ideas.  For the bento newbie (like me), it is a nice intro/launching guide to preparing great bentos.  The information on what can be made in advanced, and how long certain items will last in the fridge or freezer makes it very easy to start planning your bentos out.  As a cookbook, I feel it lacks a bit in recipes overall.  As a reference for ideas and meals (especially packed lunches for work), I think it will inspire a few more creations and occasionally be thumbed through for a recipe.  It is in no way a `go to' reference cookbook, but it does not seem to be the intention.  The general feel of this book makes the thought of preparing bentos far less intimidating, which I would assume is the goal.  For that reason I would say I am overall happy with the purchase, but I wouldn't likely replace my copy again.

B004RLPWXS|4.0|Ever since I got this, I haven't been able to stop listening to three of the tracks in particular.  I've been a Duran fan off and on for years (my favorite was the Wedding Album and, of course, the back '80s catalog) and listening to this took me right back.  The slinky bomp-bomp-bomp bass is there that featured so prominently in so many albums released in the 80s (the early 80s especially), with a layer of electronica laid over some of the tracks that, thankfully, doesn't detract from the richness of the sound.<br /><br />One thing I'll give the guys, here: Simon LeBon admitted once a long while ago that when he would write lyrics for the band in the 80s, he just strung meaningless sentences together because they sounded good (and, boy, can you tell if you actually listen to them).  It seems as though he's made more of an effort to break that habit, this time around, because even though the music is like someone took a ball of silly putty, rubbed it on their albums from the 80s and copied the sound as faithfully as possible, the lyrics have a stronger narrative structure, but all the wonderful dramatic flair and weirdness they did before, without sacrificing any coherency.<br /><br />Three tracks you should listen to before you listen to any of the others: \\"Leave A Light On\\", \\"The Man Who Stole a Leopard\\" and \\"Before the Rain\\" - these tracks are the ones I'm thinking about in particular when I think about the album's strong points (which I've mentioned above).  \\"Before the Rain\\" sounds more like a missing track from \\"The Wedding Album\\", much darker than the other two, more circumspect (to the point of almost sounding bitter), though all three of them generally veer away from the hyperkinetic bounce of the other tracks on the album.  \\"The Man Who Stole a Leopard\\" is just about as perfect a song as you can possibly ask for, especially from these guys, who've had so many perfect singles and album tracks.  The fusion of faithfully-rendered pulsing 80s synths with delicate strains of violin mix works remarkably well.  The lyrics are wonderfully sexy, the story is intriguing, and the fact that they got Kelis (of dubious \\"Milkshake\\" fame) to sing the leopard's answering refrains shows that DD don't feel threatened by the new crop of artists out there.  Seriously, this song is perfect.  \\"Leave A Light On\\" is a lovely blend of guitar and synth, with a chorus that's full of yearning and promise all at the same time.  Another up-side is just how wonderful Simon's voice sounds - as sweet as ever, which makes \\"Leave a Light On\\" (and \\"Leopard\\", too, actually) even better.<br /><br />The rest of the album can't stand up to these three tracks, sadly.  The remaining tracks lament (as many of their songs have from the last few albums -- \\"Too Much Information\\", anyone?) the new celebutainment culture we find ourselves buried up to the neck in these days and the frustration and interference that the convenience of social media and technology can exact as its price (just because you CAN share everything about yourself on the web, does that mean you necessarily SHOULD?), mixed in with a few of the old 'you sexy chicks are so sexy!' songs.  I have to say, though, the conceit of \\"Blame the Machines\\" is a cute idea, cleverly executed.  \\"Girl Panic\\" is one of the few tracks that really comes close to matching the brilliance of \\"...Leopard,\\" \\"Leave a Light On\\" and \\"Before the Rain\\", with fantastic harmonies and music that makes you want to move.  \\"Return to Now\\" is a stand-out instrumental interlude, that I really, REALLY wish had been longer - the recognizable melody borrowed from another song on the album rendered in string-section format is surprisingly beautiful and moving - I dare say, whoever was responsible for arranging that track should think about doing an entire album of Duran songs in such a fashion.  It'd be gorgeous.<br /><br />All in all, the album has its hits and its misses, but take my word on the three songs I mentioned.  Download those and you won't be able to stop listening to them.stop listening to them.

B00005NKKV|5.0|This was the defining American album of the mid 1960's. It was a time when England was calling all the shots in music, Motown was not yet a real force, and most of the alternatives to the British Invasion were Roy Orbison, Neil Sedaka and the Righteous Brothers. Simon and Garfunkel spoke directly to the loneliness of the soul that was pre-Woodstock adolescence, and the rejection of the men in their gray flannel suits who were propelling us to be things we did not want to be anymore. Perhaps this is why Simon and Garfunkel were so despised by the adults of the time.   This was the innocent and poetic Paul Simon before he studied music formally, and they sucked out his living soul and left him the insipid husk that would write trash like \\"Call Me Al\\" and \\"Slip-Slidin' Away\\".   Art Garfunkel has the voice of an angel, and apparently the heart of one too. His brief foray into acting (Catch 22, Carnal Knowledge) soon turned him to pursue a deeper and more meaningful life than his shallow and egotistical partner, as he dedicated himself to helping children.  Simon and Garfunkel would bless the world shortly after this album with the lively and deceptively deep soundtrack to The Graduate and propel the acting career of a virtual unknown named Dustin Hoffman.  But in 1965 and '66, we felt a chill autumn wind blowing across us as our older classmates graduated and were drafted and went to a frightening and forbidding place called Vietnam, where they were offered as blood sacrifices to the political ambitions of the greedy, hound-faced President Lyndon Johnson. And we truly felt the Sounds of Silence.

B00003JRCQ|5.0|This \\"Persuasion\\" is the standard by which all other adaptations of britlit should be judged. Amanda Root is flawless as the faded and resigned Anne who has already accepted her fate as spinster aunt and put-upon companion to her uncaring family. Ciaran Hinds wonderfully brings to life Captain Wentworth who made his fortune during the Napoleonic sea battles and is now ready to settle down. They are both tragic victims of the standards of the time,  separated while in their early twenties by class consciousness and lack of money (his). But the Fates have destined that they meet again years later and despite the wounded pride of the Captain, and Anne's fragile feelings that threaten to overcome her at a moment's notice, they do find each other once more.    This is a movie about hidden passions: about Anne's repressed and fragile feelings, and about Frederick's channeled passions and wounded pride which have become duty to king and country.  Jane Austen's brilliance lay in using words to imply the bubbling cauldron of emotions beneath the surface of good manners and this movie shows these passions in a rapid glance, a trembling hand on a chair, a guarded comment. But beneath the happy story is the sadness we feel when we realize that fictional Anne is just one of thousands of real young women who had been destined to remain spinsters at home, burdens to be tolerated but not loved. Anne is lucky and we know it.    The mastery of this film (including its acting, costumes, lighting, direction, and even its slow pace) is seeing ghostly Anne change from a pale shadow that haunts her family home, wearing washed out colors, going through her days unloved and unwanted, to a young woman who literally wakes up to life and love, a bloom in her cheeks as she herself blossoms into the passionate woman she always has been under her sadness. She alone of her family is deserving of this monumental and deep love and we are happy for her.

From beginning to end, this is easily the best Duran Duran album since 1988's Big Thing (and to many, it may go further back than that - I was always quite partial to Big Thing).  I know most people know the story of Mark Ronson being a long time Duran Duran fan and wanting to make the imaginary follow-up to Rio, so I'll skip that and get into the album itself.    First single and title track \\"All You Need Is Now\\" almost sounds like two completely different songs.  The verses have an electro-whirring almost industrial sound that makes you wonder on first listen what the hell is going on...until the song reaches it's \\"New Moon On Monday\\"-ish chorus.  This is the first point on the CD that you realize that \\"yeah, they've still got it\\".  After a few listens though, the entire track rather seemlessly melds together into a tune you'll have a hard time getting out of your head.    \\"Blame The Machines\\" and \\"Being Followed\\" both sound like they could have been at home on the band's first album (with \\"Being Followed\\" - which sounds like an offspring of \\"Careless Memories\\" being the stronger of the two in my opinion).  Next up is the son of \\"Save a Prayer\\" called \\"Leave A Light On\\".  It's the one song on the original 9-track download release that sounds somewhat forced and calculated - and, not surprisingly, my candidate for weakest song on the original download.    After that minor hiccup, the far more interesting second half of the CD starts up with an appearance from Ana Matronic of the Scissor Sisters on \\"Safe (In The Heat of the Moment)\\".  It's a throwback funk disco number complete with cowbells - yes, cowbells - and slap bass where Simon's more animated vocal juxtaposes well with the cool come on's from Ana.    \\"Girl Panic!\\" is next and if this doesn't deserve to be a radio staple this Spring, I don't know what does.  Take one part \\"Girls on Film\\", one part \\"Planet Earth\\", one part \\"Rio\\" and the best pop vocal Simonhas laid down in years and you get this highly uptempo dance floor gem that I can't wait to hear live.    \\"The Man Who Stole A Leopard\\" is next and do yourself a favor - just listen to it.  There's a reason why people are raving about this one.  Yes, it has a very \\"The Chauffeur\\" feel to it.  Yes, it has (a surprisingly restrained) Kelis singing with Simon.  It's also incredibly unique and even clocking in at over six minutes, it's hands down the best song on the album.    Rounding out the album are the \\"Last Chance on the Stairway\\"-ish \\"Runway Runaway\\" (and it's kick ass chorus) and moody closer \\"Before the Rain\\".    Alas, this release has been expanded from the original download version released in December.  Having just gotten the expanded release today, these are my initial impressions of the added tracks.  \\"A Diamond In The Mind\\" and \\"Return to Now\\" are a pair of orchestral interludes (much in the vein of \\"Interlude One\\" and \\"Flute Interlude\\" on Big Thing).  \\"Too Bad You're So Beautiful\\" is the best of the added tracks with it's uptempo groove.  \\"Mediterranea\\" is a slow mover which to me is going to be that song that ultimately grows on you with repeated listens.  \\"Other People's Lives\\" is D2 going political (passable but not memorable) and \\"Networker Nation\\" borders on filler.  Additionally, the limited release available at Best Buy adds two more songs to the release - \\"Too Close To The Sun\\" (filler) and \\"Early Summer Nerves\\" (a midtempo track with a sneaky catchy quality to it).    In summary, the boys are back.  Sure, they're paying homage...but it's to their own storied past.  With noticable influences of some of the best songs they wrote back in the 80's, Duran Duran have made a record capable of being placed alongside their best work.  After a few decades wandering around with hits and misses and with a little prodding from a producer that gave them the push they needed, Duran Duran are finally back in the territory they helped to create...and doing it better than anyone had a right to expect.

0817439390|4.0|Bryan Peterson's Understand Exposure is an outstanding introductory text for new photographers and along with Scott Kelby's Digital Photography 3 volume book set was my beginning point to learning my new hobby.  With literally hundreds of books to choose from, I think you would be hard-pressed to find a better book to help you conquer one's fear of unlocking the potential of your camera and getting away from shooting in the Program mode.    Bryan outlines the 3 components that go into making up an exposure(picture):  Aperture, shutter speed and ISO.  He relates how you can pair aperture and shutter speed to get a properly exposed picture and then addresses both aperture and shutter speed in detail to explain how to obtain the \\"creative\\" exposure you are looking to achieve, often with comparison photos to demonstrate his points.  This is the strength of the book and made the purchase worthwhile, it's also the reason for my 5-star rating.    Great photos are seen throughout the book with focal lengths, f/stops and shutter speeds listed along w/ the captions for each photo.     He briefly covers different types of continuous lighting, his techniques to help get an exposure: The Sky Brothers and Mr. Green Jeans and rounds the book out with a brief description of filters, flash & HDR.    The only question I have, after viewing the Table of Contents for Bryan's Understanding Photography Field Guide, the same material seems to be included, although the accompanying photos may not, I am left with the question of whether I may have better off having purchased that book instead.  Needless to say, his Field Guide is on my list of purchases to make, and will try to update this after taking possession of it and Digital Exposure Handbook by Ross Hoddinott, which is currently on order.    Update: Ross Hoddinott's Digital Exposure Handbook has a little more information and is equally as good at explaining and demonstrating the keys to exposure as Understanding Exposure.  I still plan on purchasing Bryan's Field Guide, which I have a feeling makes Understanding Exposure unnecessary.

B004QKNRLE|5.0|The minds of people approaching the age of 60 are supposed to be invaded and captured by a first wave of childhood memories. It prompts self-centered Max Morden (MM), age unknown, to write this quite frank and transparent autobiography. So frank that after reading it, two dozen adjectives are insufficient to fully describe MM's own character, whose book is situated on the Irish coast with a view of the sea. Readers might want to make a tally of negative adjectives on MM, and compare them in book reviewing gatherings.<br /><br />MM is an elderly writer on French painters (a dilettante, second-rater in his own understated judgment), still quite tall, but in mental and physical decline. After the death of his wife Anna he has returned to where all his early life-stirring events and drama began, to The Cedars, the rented house the far wealthier Clare family occupied many decades ago. As a tall 11-year old he befriended the smaller, strange twins Myles and Chloe, fell in love with their mother but only weeks later became enchanted with Chloe. Now The Cedars has become a rundown lodging house with an eccentric landlady and only one other occupant of its six rooms for rent, a retired Colonel with set habits. The atmosphere!<br /><br />From a young age MM has been an acute watcher and every page provides evidence. In later life, when early memories crop up unaided, MM often links incoming thoughts and images to paintings he has studied and adores, Bonnard most of all (and de la Tour and Gericault, as MM would like me to add to this review). Late in the autobiography MM admits he has no personality, never had one. It may be the key admission in this brilliantly-paced, tragic and often hilariously-funny account of the life of a person unable to relate to anyone.<br /><br />John Banville won the 2005 Man Booker Prize with this masterpiece, which is perfectly paced, with surprises hidden throughout the book right until the final pages. On every page the reader is challenged to pick up a dictionary to acknowledge MM's Irish superior way with words. An absolutely great, rich and annually re-readable novel. And the gap of five or more decades between MM's youthful dreaming and his elderly self are for the reader to fabulate about.acknowledge MM's Irish superior way with words. 1594202842|2.0|Of the thousands of impassioned comments that hit the WSJ website following its publishing of the infamous &#34;Are Chinese Mothers Superior?&#34; excerpt, one of the most interesting ones came from an Asian woman who said she was an undergraduate at Harvard the same time as Amy Chua.  She was not a big fan of Chua, observing that Chua's unsavory personality appears unchanged from her college days.  She also remembered that while at Harvard, Chua showed little interest in taking part in activities or clubs aimed at Asian American students.<br /><br />While there's always the chance that this was the kind of fabrication that often appears on anonymous message boards, it rang true to me.  Here's an Asian archetype Amy Chua should consider: the upper middle class, educated Asian person who grew up in the United States, speaks perfect English, acts totally American and would barely know how to ask where the bathroom was if you dropped them into their parents' Asian homeland and who recoils from doing something as un-cool as hanging around lots of other Asians, but opportunistically trots out their alleged Asian-ness to impress their upper middle class white peers.<br /><br />I suspect that's what's behind one of the more annoying aspects of this book: Chua's constant invoking of &#34;Chinese&#34;-ness as if that were something she embodies and has the authority to define.  That's a real stretch coming from someone who is so plainly American in accent, demeanor, and life experience (watching one of her TV interviews for around 5 seconds reveals this).  The thing is, she undoubtedly knows this, being the extremely intelligent woman that she is.  So her constant invoking of the &#34;Chinese way&#34; and &#34;Chinese parenting&#34; seems like deliberate, cynical pandering to stereotypes that she believes American readers will lap up.<br /><br />In one strange passage in the book, she complains how some white men have Asian fetishes and will only date Asian women (Chua adds that these men do this even when the Asian women in question are not good-looking, which strikes her as particularly objectionable).  Meanwhile, she sings the praises of her accomplished husband and his Jewish background, proudly noting that Jewish man-Asian woman couples are ubiquitous in affluent communities such as hers.  This whole subject is worthy of a book in itself, but suffice it to say that this all reminded me of the personals ads I used to come across where Asian American women declared, &#34;I will not be your geisha girl!&#34; or &#34;If you're expecting a submissive, meek Asian woman, look elsewhere!&#34;, but then specified &#34;Asian Woman Seeks White Man&#34; in the headline.  I suspect that a truly &#34;Chinese&#34; person might not exhibit these types of internal conflicts.<br /><br />Despite her vigorous backpedaling during her PR campaign for the book, throughout &#34;Battle Hymn&#34;, Chua comes off as monomaniacal and abusive toward her daughters amidst her odd quest to make them into world-class musicians.  On some level, she may have a point:  it's true that you're not going to play at Carnegie Hall if you only practice 15 minutes a day and play with your friends all the time.  Then again, as so many have pointed out in the uproar surrounding this book, should &#34;success&#34; be defined only by the very narrow metrics Chua embraces?  Even if we do go by the popular measure of material wealth, I know that out in the business world, many (most?) extremely successful people were not child prodigies and did not experience anything close to &#34;Tiger Mom&#34; parenting, while plenty of Asian Americans with stereotypically domineering parents have not met with the same success, chosen predictable and somewhat dull careers, and remain resentful about their childhoods.<br /><br />The last few chapters of the book were a revelation.  Chua, in painful detail, writes about her younger daughter's &#34;rebellion&#34; and how her kids accuse her of being a &#34;freak&#34; and of only caring about herself.  Chua writes about her own father's estrangement from his parents due to *their* domineering parenting style.  She recounts how one of her students discovers some of the detailed practice instructions she wrote up for her daughters and is surprised at how his affable professor could ever be responsible for something so &#34;weird&#34;.<br /><br />In other words, all this criticism that everyone's thrown at Amy Chua in the aftermath of the WSJ article...she has heard it all before.  We are not telling her anything she doesn't know already.  How this ultra-smart, worldly, completely American woman can hear all these compelling criticisms yet remain so oblivious to the dark side of this style of raising kids is one of the big mysteries of this book. 1591843790|5.0|In his quick-witted and user-friendly way of speaking, Guy Kawasaki, in Enchantment, does a fine job in getting you from theory to practice. He outlines the conceptual pieces of being enchanting, or having influence, in a way that people can grasp immediately. Then he leads you to the implementation of the concepts and practices and how they apply to your business. Without saying, it is clear Guy knows business is about people.  You quickly see where the `hot' points are to connect with people, whether it's your employees or your boss.<br /><br />As a specialist on influence and women, there are several key pieces that I have found to be especially valuable to women. He touches on the concept of likeability, which is a continuous issue for women ... who are looking for respect, wanting to be valued for their ideas, and have many gifts to give. Guy, touches on the following points on enchantment, which I would like to describe from the woman's point of view.<br /><br />The first main point Guy makes is likeability. Women wrestle with the distinction of being likeable versus the need to be liked. This presents a dilemma for women because women are conditioned to please and do for others. When women want to be liked they may exhibit behavior that is not necessarily appropriate in the workplace. It's about being professional not flirty or overly agreeable. Guy says, \\"do what your boss tells you to do. The relationship will develop from there, giving you the opportunity to express yourself while maintaining respect and likeability.\\"<br /><br />Guy talks about making a good first impression, your smile, dress, handshake and vocabulary. It's the vocabulary or language that women need to pay attention to.  Corporate and business language is fairly military-based. It's the language of kill or be killed - strategy, execution, win, competition, and tactics. Is that natural for women? No. But it is something women need to recognize in how the language they use affects the respect or validation they get. In order to get close to someone you need to use the language they use.<br /><br />At the same time, Guy talks about getting close to people you want to enchant, he discusses the role of technology. Technology has allowed women to form more communities. Look at the vast numbers of women on the social media sites, sharing, supporting and building relationships. Social media has opened up a whole new dimension which women are using to be heard.<br /><br />Another point Guy makes about likeability is to know and share your passion. This comes naturally to women. Especially when we are encouraged to talk about how we feel, what we know in our gut and how we interpret information. Women are extremely successful in creating win-win situations.<br /><br />Trustworthiness is Guy's second main point of being enchanting. Trusting others is the first step in becoming trustworthy. Women need to make trust in their private relationships a cornerstone. We need to also do the same in the workplace. An example is, how female bullies in the workplace violate trust in their relationships. The growing issue around female bullying is an example of how not to be likeable or trustworthy.<br /><br />Guy includes in Enchantment a short step-by-step way to position yourself for becoming likeable and gaining trust when giving your 30 second elevator pitch about you and your business. Women find this easy because we are closely in touch with our feelings. We aren't afraid of introspection and expressing. This is one of our advantages in becoming enchanters.<br /><br />Another part of Enchantment I found useful, on the whole for women, is how to overcome resistance. In particular, the part about framing competition. Woman are terrible in identifying who their real adversaries are. We need to think about who we trust and how that's built. We need to do a better job in how we perceive and truthfully express what and who the competition is.<br /><br />I think one section women will enjoy and possibly relate to is on how to resist enchantment. Guy talks about noticing fake and phony enchanters. He urges us to recognize when the enchanter is not acting with integrity. This is when the book gets interesting as we've all been in situations where we've liked and trusted another person and have been hurt. Guy addresses this by saying it's unethical to enchant the gullible person since they are prone to vulnerability. And the person who enchants, and doesn't have their best interest at heart is not ethical by any stretch of the imagination. His solution? Stay away from dishonest people who your gut says aren't trustworthy.<br /><br />Guy's book, Enchantment, does a great job of raising points that women can use as guides to becoming powerful enchanters.hantment. I do NOT turn off my data connection for unobtrusive ads.    So I would recommend you give this a miss, stick with the free version & simply turn off your data when you play. 0307477479|4.0|I can see why Jennifer Egan's latest book's been racking up the prizes, most recently the National Book Critics Circle Award.  It's beautifully crafted at a sentence by sentence level, expansive in scope - spanning the continent and over 50 years' of our recent history, and wildly various in its exploration of the inner worlds of many diverse characters.    Summing it up feels nearly impossible, because this novel's about what all great novels are about: nothing short of the nature of human beings, our choices, our challenges, our idiosyncrasies, our failings, and our occasional moments of grace.  Egan takes it all on, and we cruise or drift or hurtle along with her as one character after another takes the stage.  She adeptly shifts perspective among these loosely connected people, exploring who they are as their paths briefly cross, weaving a web of connection that anchors the book.  Ultimately, though, it's more like that famous abstract Pollock painting hanging in the Metropolitan than a roller-coaster ride: equally thrilling, but in a single panel rather than a flow.  Or perhaps it's more like a symphony heard live at Avery Fischer Hall:  each instrument, each character cleanly addressing your ear while their combined forward movement creates an ineffable impression replete with drama, beauty, and pathos.    I was reminded often of Colum McCann's Let The Great World Spin, another polyphonic novel set mainly in NYC.  Egan's book spans more time (about half a century) and more places (SF as well, and even Italy and Africa, briefly) but offers a similar building-up of a world and a social network so richly conveyed that you feel you're right there breathing the atmosphere of these settings alongside the characters.  And not only does Egan employ a variety of viewpoints, she also bends form a bit:  My favorite chapter offered a series of PowerPoint slides created as a visual-verbal diary by a young girl who is the child of one of the central characters.  It providesa unique reading experience while offering humor and subtlety and social commentary all at once.  And, in fact, the whole PowerPoint is available on Ms. Egan's website: [...]    In my view, this novel - or series of interwoven short fictions, as some describe it - deserves all the accolades it's getting.  I recommend it to you with confidence.

B0006PV5U8|5.0|I will keep this short, and start by saying that I am a classical violinist who learned Sibelius and Glazunov while listening to these recordings, among others. The recording quality is outstanding on all 3 concertos, and I have no problem with the voicing of anything. I feel that the recordings are somewhat idealized for the orchestra, and for these concertos that means dark and smooth. That might just be the instruments used, but I doubt it. Regardless, it gives an amazing backdrop that I have yet to find an equal of for Heifetz.<br /><br />Heifetz of course is Heifetz, and as the other reviewers said, these recordings do nothing if not showcase his bow technique. If the orchestra is smooth and dark, he is harsh and dark. He has easily one of the most messy bow techniques of anyone ever, but he finds a way to make it work beyond belief. In any case, he rarely misses anything, and neither does the orchestra. One reviewer pointed out 1 time where the orchestra was not together, and I laughed a little inside. There are many times when the orchestra is not perfect, and when Heifetz leads them by a mile. But that is what makes these so special. This is not some mix of 15 different takes, this is the real thing. We are shown as close to a live performance as you can get to I think, considering the outstanding quality. There are mistakes, but they are turned into marvelous musical interpretation.<br /><br />My teacher, who received 3 degrees from Juliard back in its day, was talking to me about the Glazunov recording. He said he loved to slow it down and listen to all the places where Heifetz messes up, and as he says this he's glowing with excitement. I think this sums up these recordings well. No matter if you choose to appreciate them or not, you cannot deny what Heifetz does for people.

1439107955|4.0|I don't think that the difficulty in writing \\"A Biography of Cancer\\" can be overstated, yet Siddhartha Mukherjee manages to pull it off with a flair and comprehensiveness that is truly admirable.     The book takes the reader from ancient beliefs about cancer through the most recent methods in diagnosis and treatment. This is potentially very dry material, but Mukherjee has a real talent for focusing on the big picture and not getting lost in a sea of distracting details. He is able to discuss the basic biology behind the development and growth of cancer, as well as how current cancer therapies do their jobs. He also outlines the more global overall thinking on cancer, its causes, and the best strategies for treatment. This serves to highlight more general tendencies of the medical community to bind tightly to concepts until the next paradigm-shifting discovery comes along, which is usually first met with deep suspicion followed by growing acceptance. Somehow he manages to cover these subjects while conveying a real compassion for cancer patients and the challenges they face.     The only shortcoming of the book is an acceptable one. Because Mukherjee himself does research on hematological cancers such as leukemia, there is a slant of the book in that direction at the cost of missing some of the greatest cancer stories in recent years (ie the identification of HPV and the development of a vaccine to prevent cervical cancer). Nonetheless, his knowledge of the field as a whole is prodigious and truly commendable.     I give this book a strong recommend for anyone who wants a fundamental understanding of cancer(s) and how scientists approach the problems of prevention and cure.

0553582038|4.0|It's no secret that a Feast for Crows is by a large margin generally the least loved novel of the Song of Ice and Fire series (so far). A few things lead to people having this opinion that others have expressed in great detail, so i'll simply note them off as such: Firstly, and usually the strongest source of disdain, is that the book is missing the majority of the most popular POVs (Tyrion, Jon, Dany, etc). In addition to this it features very prominently two POVs that many people find a chore to read (Cersei and Briene) as well as many seeming extraneous POVs that get similar amounts of hate (All those of the Iron Islands and Dorne). Next, coming directly from the insane amount of events that rocked the aSoIaF world in Storm of Swords, this more brooding book can seem lacking in an exciting plot, with characters meandering.   But, of course, one of the main reasons people dislike this book is the fact that it was all there was after Storm for ten years. For anyone who got immersed in the books (and it's obvious many, many people did) the lack of resolutions or even continuations of so much of the plot in this book coupled with waiting year after year for a Dance with Dragons created a lot of negative emotions. Because of the lack of any continuation the decided inconclusive nature of Feast made many dislike it even more.  Now, I understand all these opinions (and heck, I even share a few of them too). But I still loved A Feast for Crows. Hell, it's tied for my favorite aSoIaF book (with Storm, duh). Especially now with Dance so soon to be released it's becoming easier and easier to appreciate all the great qualities of this book. Where others see a slow moving plot, I see great world building; where some hate to read nigh-insane Cersei and \\"boring\\" Brienne I see two of the better characters in the series coming into the limelight. This book is indeed slower than the others, and at times stumbles in execution (the beginning is not up to GRRM's writing abilities, Arya's story is not as captivating as she should be) but Feast has some of Martin's best writing in the entire series. And great events do happen- they're just more subtle then they were in previous books. I'd talk about them here, but I don't want to give spoilers- let's just say many of the reveals are fantastic. And can I mention that both Jaime and Sansa are two of the best POV's in the series?   In the end Feast has aspects that can be frustrating, but I really think the positive vastly outweighs the negatives. And with Dance soon to be arriving at our doorsteps, fulfilling so many of the characters we've been missing and (hopefully) answering many questions we've been asking for years, I think Feast will soon be seen through a new perspective.

Summer Wars is a full-length feature blending in high school romance with digital armageddon.<br /><br />At the beginning of summer vacation in Japan, math whiz Kenji is working with his buddy Takashi as a moderator for the massive online network called OZ(no Wizard Of Oz puns present)which is a virtual world similar to WOW, but with less of a midevil setting and more like playful icons involved in fighter tournaments. Kenji's cute energetic classmate Natsuki offers him another quick job to help her prepare for her great-gramma's 90th birthday. He accepts, and the two of them go off to Natsuki's family estate in the country. They are a large and diverse family descended from a samurai clan, and the great-grandmother Sakae is about everything you'd be looking for in granny. Kenji is shocked though when Natsuki claims to her family that he is her boyfriend, but she says that in order to make her grandma feel safe about her. Confused during the night, he gets a strange text message send in code, which he uses his math skills to crack, and sends it back. He awakes the next morning to see on the news that OZ has been been hacked into and that he is being blamed for it. It turns out that an A.I. program masquerading as Kenji's avatar, calling itself Love Machine. It proceeds to take over OZ, which Kenji tries to stop with a new avatar Takashi made for him, along with Natsuki's cousin who is really the brilliant game fighter, King Kazma. They tangle with Love Machine, but after absorbing a few other avatars, it goes all Super-Saiyan and kicks them out of the system. It then proceeds to infect computer systems all over the world, causing mass confusion and hysteria. Its later revealed that Natsuki's estranged uncle is the one who actually created the A.I. that the American military try to use, but it escaped into OZ. This news infuriates Sakae as she banishes him from her home. The ensuing action causes Sakae to pass away in her sleep. The family believes that Love Machine is responsible, so they pull all the stops to try and pull the plug on it. They call in resouces from all over the country, even by bringing in a huge supercomputer to back them up. Natsuki uses her superior skills at tea ceremony card playing to challenge Love Machine to a final card game. She gets help from players all over the world to shut him down. However, it causes a satellite to begin falling towards their location. Kenji hacks into the satellite's system, and has it crash near the estate.<br /><br />Summer Wars features some impressive digital effects, specifically in the online world, although you sorta wish there was more added to it than the cartoonish avatars. (As a side note, why does EVERYTHING have to be a trilogy these days?  Is it for marketing reasons?  Most stories only need one book in order to get the point across, and everything else just ruins the impact of the first installment.  Maggie Stiefwater, I'm looking at you.)    So anyway... in case I can't finish, I'm going to say now what I don't like about this book.  As a couple of other reviewers have said, this is simply not an effective dystopian novel.  In order to create a powerful dystopia, the author must convey to the reader that the world she describes could evolve out of current science and social or political trends.  That's what makes a dystopia frightening and what makes the reader think... the point of the dystopian genre is to get the audience to consider what will happen to us if we continue down the road we're traveling now.    Lauren Oliver fails at this.  In fact, I don't get the sense that she even attempted to do this or that she has any concept of the dystopian genre other than that it describes a society that is \\"bad.\\"  I feel no connection to the world described in the book.  I can't imagine any society evolving to the point of eradicating love, of all things, so I can't take it seriously and it's having no impact on me. There's little discussion of the \\"science\\" behind being able to \\"cure\\" love, and I need a hefty dose of fringe science in my dystopian novels in order to make them believable.  It's also unrealistic to think that most kids wouldn't have the experience of falling in love before the age of 18.  I mean, duh. I don't care how much brainwashing they've undergone.  Kids today are having sex at 12 and 13.  Whatever.  As for any \\"lessons\\" that might come out of a story about messing with people's brains and emotions (i.e. the side effects of eradicating things like friendship, sexual preference, familial love, and even a passion for work or hobbies, which to the author's credit are at least given nominal mention a few chapters into the book) - well, I think America actually *learned* all those lessons during the period in which we lobotomized thousands of mentally ill, depressed, and anxious patients, insomniacs, and people with learning disabilities.  The book has a been there, done that feel to me, and I feel no emotional connection and none of the terror that comes with a truly powerful dystopia (for example, Brave New World, We, 1984, The Handmaid's Tale, Oryx and Crake, Lord of the Flies, and more recently, the Hunger Games and Across the Universe.)    It seems to me that the \\"dystopian\\" element in this novel is just a convenient way to set up the same old tired \\"Bella and Edward rebel against a world that can't understand their super speshul relationship\\" story.  If you like that type of story, this book is for you.  If you're considering picking up this book because you like dystopian or speculative fiction, then there are plenty of better places to look.

B001IF252M|1.0|** I bought mine from xcessbuy; the following review MIGHT not apply to the other sellers... But be aware there are knockoffs out there! **    I bought this hoping it would be the official Apple one; but alas, it is a knockoff. This is only obvious when you put it next to the real thing and it's visibly wider and with a longer, cheaper-made cord than the official Apple adapter. But in functionality, you might have problems and I would advise you to rethink risking your money to save a few dollars.    Part of the VGA standard is that devices can transfer an EDID, an identifier telling the computer about the screen's possible resolutions and so on. Some displays do not transfer this EDID; usually they are older, but sometimes this is done on purpose, for example the projectors at my school do not send the EDID on purpose (according to the IT guy).    Why does this matter? The knockoff cable won't detect a screen if it doesn't send an EDID. I bought this for the specific purpose of presenting in my classes, only to find it didn't detect the projector. I went home and it worked on my Dell LCD monitor (it's pretty old) and my little Toshiba TV (I think 18\\"). The firmware update mentioned in another comment didn't detect this adapter at all because it's a fake; looking in System Profiler, the knockoff's firmware version is \\"0.00\\" so it doesn't even pretend to be an official Apple one.    I still needed to hook my MBP to the projectors at school, so I broke down and drove to my local Apple store, where I purchased a real MB572Z/A adapter. I then went straight to a classroom to try it, and it worked flawlessly with the projector system. I double-checked and indeed the knockoff did nothing and the Apple adapter just worked, every time.    I didn't bother trying to return this knockoff cord, I'll keep it for use at home and then use the Apple one everywhere else. But I learned my lesson... In trying to save a few dollars, I ended up wasting money and having to buy the real thing in the end. Don't make the same mistake. Just buy the real thing straight from Apple, unless you know for sure that your screens send the EDID.    (MacBook Pro 17\\" mid-2010)

B000QQFKYE|5.0|If you haven't found the slightly concealed pinpoint \\"entrance\\", then this will only appear to be a frantic, hysterical torrent of impenetrable paranoia. BUT, if you are fortunate or clever enough to decipher the Polish characters \\"instructions\\" at the very beginning of how exactly to \\"enter\\", then it transforms into a thrillingly visceral roller coaster ride through the vast caverns, ornate galleries and cramped cupboards of David Lynch's irresistibly Gothic mind. The story that you then experience -- and it is indeed a profound, shatteringly intense experience -- unfolds with a stunningly vivid, logical rationale.<br /><br />The \\"key\\" is in grasping that David is first requiring us to make a very definite adjustment to our \\"awareness\\", to move towards something like a meditative state where \\"time\\" and \\"space\\" are more fluid, less absolute entities. You MUST release yourself from a strictly literal reading or interpretation of the events and locales and identities, and then somehow, magically, it suddenly makes so much more sense. In fact, it turns out to be Lynch's most intensely sensible creation. That's the perplexing nature of it's remarkable method, its total dependence on the paradoxical quality of its form. Its assimilation of \\"Eastern\\" modes of consciousness in order to reveal a genuinely staggering insight into our \\"Western\\" culture can be seen as a parallel of David's attainment of his own personal enlightenment. David is a long time practitioner of Transcendental Meditation and as such has acquired formidable skill at separating himself from his temporal existence and connecting with an infinitely more intense, more accurate, more valuable reality. He is working in all his art, and especially here, to have us also acquainted with this vast, infinite experience. That's the reason for the seemingly impossible structure, and the secret to gaining entrance. At first attempt I didn't understand how to \\"enter\\" and I was frustrated, perplexed, pissed off with David's seemingly arrogant self involvement. But I thought, \\"No, I must be missing something...\\" so I went back to the beginning and that's when I discovered the barely concealed directions for gaining entry.<br /><br />You may be one of those Lynch fan's that believes that it is intentionally irrational, obtuse and inscrutable, and not in any way meant to be rational, but are non the less impressed and complimentary. Well, unfortunately you have not experienced the real \\"inner\\" story and should go back and try again. Unless your experience is remarkably lucid, and intensely coherent, and thoroughly devastating, then you've not gotten \\"in\\". You are only marveling at the intimidating exterior appearance of Lynch's formidable vehicle. You have not yet actually ridden it. You'll know it when you're \\"in\\", believe me. There's no confusing this. I know exactly how it feels to be on the outside looking in, and how that in no way is this movie's ultimate intent. If anything, the false starts only made my eventual \\"admission\\" all that more meaningful and intense. If you've not experienced it as I have, as a remarkably efficient and elegant conveyor of a profoundly intense experience, then you have missed out.<br /><br />David has exploited all of his previous film, TV, internet, musical, and visual arts in creating an entirely new art, but it's distinct, separate, unlike anything else he's previously created, perhaps anyone else. Don't compare this to Mulholland Drive, Eraserhead, Twin Peaks, or even to his webcast videos. This is entirely its own beast. David's web series Rabbits are a deconstruction of the Western narrative form that, through Film Noir, emerged as a compelling means to convey our innate sense of deep anxiety, confusion and terror of confronting the mysteries of our reality. Rabbits resembles a TV sitcom, a Broadway drama, an off Broadway absurdist experiment, or even a video parody of all of the above, and in Inland Empire the Rabbits take on a \\"sacred\\" role as supernatural icons of our media infused culture. Our persistent suspicion about life is fertile ground for some extremely curious hybrid art. Paranoia is a potent muse.<br /><br />Very often, at key moments, INLAND EMPIRE plays like a horribly trite soap opera. Simultaneously, it feels like a meandering documentary, never quite focusing on any one single topic. And just as often it seems content to hover in an undefined, unlabeled space, not concerned with its name or purpose. None of this is gratuitous, random, arbitrary. It's all supremely relevant, essential. Ultimately, it feels as though there isn't a disposable scene, an inconsequential moment. It's quite lengthy if you're just \\"watching\\" it, but once I managed to get \\"on track\\" it soared past me, around me, through me at a blistering pace. At one point towards the conclusion I somehow was thrown off, I found myself as lost and confused as I had been initially. I stopped and rewound the video to the point of confusion, and sat and considered everything that had just transpired, absorbing as much as possible, and reconnecting with the film's powerful rhythm. Again, I sensed a realignment of my awareness as a fuller sense of the action coalesced into a tangible experience, and I then resumed the video. And now the final scenes were all the more more vivid, lucid, emphatically understandable, and so completely enveloping, like superb 3D in my mind -- Ultra, Mega, Turbo 3D.<br /><br />Many viewers feel, or express a belief, that Laura Dern has turned in an extraordinary performance. I am not so sure. And what's interesting is that I believe that David was not only allowing for an inferior performance from Laura, he counted on it. This may sound absurd to her ardent fans, but I have always sensed that David's fascination with Laura is more her symbolic quality than her specific skill or talent. She is for him a substitute for Hitchcock's recurring Blond Goddess In Peril, but with a skeptical edge. David is more concerned with the image, the notion of the unattainable ice princess, and the lithe, fair skinned, blue eyed Dern functions abstractly as his Janet Leigh, Tippi Hedren, or Grace Kelly. But David has allowed the gaps, the stitched seams, the flaws in her work to show. By exposing the faults and weaknesses of Laura's acting we are granted access to a curious aspect of cinema -- it's inherently deceptive quality. We are being made aware of the synthetic, fabricated artifice that too many people have for so long accepted as the equivalent of reality. As much as it is a willful contrivance, Laura's often confused, scattered, unfocused demeanor may just as much be genuine confusion with her situation. David is depending on Laura's limited awareness of the meaning of his dialog and action, immersing her in an entirely alien world so that he may capture her authentic, true anxiety. It's because of his commitment to authenticity that David in fact deceives Laura. Regardless of his respect and fondness for her, David must exploit her -- exploit her persona, which he himself is largely responsible for -- in the name of artistic integrity. This paradox is as compelling as any other encountered within this daunting video. The scenes of Laura reciting curious dialog at a table before a near mute witness reveal an actress in the process of discovering her character's motivations, excavating deeply stored impulses in the service of illusion. While functioning in the film as a ploy to temporarily confuse the audience as to who -- which manifestation of Laura's character -- we are watching, David video recorded these scenes of her reading these 15 pages as a pretense to scrutinize her process. It's in these intensely focused episodes that Laura is most vulnerable and exposed, and I often winced and cringed at what I perceive are her genuinely awkward, awful attempts at a southern accent. It seems to me that just as he did in Wild at Heart David has allowed Laura to employ a jarringly horrible accent. It's also likely, to me, that Laura is oblivious of just how bad is her attempt. There's every reason to experience her performance as inadequate. David is relying on Laura's inferior talent to fulfill his requirements for an inferior actress, Nikki Grace, in the inferior film within the film, On High In Blue Tomorrows. Rather than ask a really fine actress to \\"play down\\" for the part of Nikki, he hired a genuinely limited actress that he could appropriately exploit. He has risked the success of the entire production on Laura's unwitting participation. Many noted director's have behaved similarly, but not nearly as cynically. David actually staged a very outrageous campaign to get Laura nominated for an Oscar for her work in this film by hiring a cow to walk down Sunset Boulevard, but I believe this was just a blatant and audacious ploy to maintain his deception of Laura. In all truth it's a despicable act, but that's Hollywood, isn't it?<br /><br />There are Doctoral Theses being written about this video, schools of thought emerging that espouse its philosophy, legions of cinephiles that are as traumatized by it as they would be a terrorist attack. All this may be justly deserved, only time will tell. But I can confidently assert that the most revolutionary aspect of Inland Empire is it's entirely unique and unprecedented cinematic method. This astonishingly powerful method is the equivalent of a mind-stilling mantra; the sound of one hand clapping...unattainable ice princess, and the lithe, fair skinned, blue eyed Dern functions abstractly as his Janet Leigh, Tippi Hedren, or Grace Kelly.
