0545123283|4.0|I dove into Linger right after finishing Shiver and once again couldn't stop reading until I had turned the last page. There is something very enchanting and addicting about these books! In Linger we are introduced to a new character, Cole, and the author also starts telling the story from both his and Isabelle's perspectives in addition to Grace and Sam's. Even though its pretty obvious where the plot is going, there's a lot of emotional struggle in this book which keeps things interesting--Grace dealing with her parents, Sam's new responsibility caring for the new wolves, and Cole caught between fleeing or facing his past.<br /><br />In Linger, the romance develops and continues to be very sweet. I really love Sam's character; he's not the dark, brooding, mysterious, bad-boy that's in practically every YA fantasy novel these days. His character is so refreshing. He's sweet, kind, chivalrous, thoughtful, sincere, sensitive, caring...he's like the sort of guy you would want to date in real life, not your 'fantasy boyfriend.'<br /><br />I was pleased to see that the author let the character's voices break through her own a little more in this book, especially with Cole and Isabelle's points of view. However there's still room for improvement. The author's writing is beautiful, but the thing about first person is it's the character telling the story with THEIR voice, not the author's. Each character will describe things differently, notice different things, etc. Not every character should describe their surroundings with the beautiful, rich language that the author uses (especially Cole and Isabelle. That style is suited more to Sam and maybe Grace in my opinion).<br /><br />One thing that began to bother me was the overuse of the phrase, \\"he/she gave ____ a withering look.\\" I lost count of how many times I came across it. This is the fault of the editor, however, not the author. It's the editor's job to catch overused phrases/words because authors tend not to be able to pick them out themselves.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book, but the main reason I have to give it four stars is because of the whole werewolf transformation theory. It's still not making sense, and things are getting even more confusing. Now the characters are beginning to question if heat and cold is what triggers the transformations at all. I think they're just as lost as I am... Hopefully things will become clearer in the next book.<br /><br />I really recommend both Shiver and Linger, and I can't wait to read Forever in July!t themselves. B0002TKFR2|3.0|Who am I writing this for? I don't care a hoot for sound-in-itself, because I'm interested in the music first, then the performance. So if you're sound buff, read the other reviews.  Same deal with Heifetz worship. he was a fiddler, not a composer. It is sheer nonsense to claim that he was \\"the greatest\\", or this recording \\"the best ever\\". These things cannot be quantified. To say such a thing is not helpful to a reader either; and we are supposed to be helpful when writing reviews.  There has always been a \\"problem\\" with Heifetz. Music lovers unacquainted with it, should be made aware that technical difficulties didn't exist for him. The outcome was that he tended to play faster than other violinists, and accordingly his recordings of the standard masterpieces (like Beethoven and Mendelssohn) may come to sound aloof, uninvolved. It is true that Heifetz had a tendency not to linger over gentle melodic lines. These are facts, not opinions. Everyone is in a position to discern them. The question is simply whether the listener feels comfortable with it or not. This is where these few lines wish to help.  Both these concertos exhibit Heifetz's urge to \\"get on with it\\". The sheer fluidity of Heifetz playing is indeed a marvel. But now I have to give my own opinion, based on something like 40 to 50 auditions of the recording and its principal rivals. I find that, in Beethoven's head movement, he does not bother to articulate the expressive episodes; and that he is strongly inclined to rush over scales running up and down, as if they were totally uninteresting - whereas Perlman for one finds plenty of honey to pour over those phrases. In certain moods you might find Heifetz's nonchalance, his refusal to dig into the music and find the greatness not his own in there, extremely irritating. The other two movements are much better in this respect - the last movement because the little tune actually sounds better on the fast side.  What I've said here about Beethoven applies inequal measure to Mendelssohn. This is not as well recorded as the other work: it is more dull, the orchestra sounds thicker and more muffled; and once or twice Heifetz's violin gives off a shriek. There is however, one issue that may tilt the balance in his favour for those who are interested in historical curiosities. For this recording Heifetz used the same violin (Khevenmuller Stradivari) on which Ferdinand David played the world premiere of the work.  I personally find these recordings indispensable as historical documents of one of the great musicians of the 20th century. But I have plenty of other recordings in my collection when I just to immerse myself in the music. If you have yet to buy your first recording of either work, then Heifetz is unlikely to be a first choice recommendation. You have know this music very well to appreiate what he is doing. But if you have other recordings and wonder what greets you here, I hope you'll get the gist of it from this short account.

0553801503|2.0|**spoilers **   What bothered me the most about the 4th entry in this series wasnt the absence of most of my favourite POV characters. I could live with that. What really grieves me is to watch a fine writer being doomed by some very serious writing syndromes. I'll try to explain some of them bellow.  *The Titus Andronicus syndrome. Titus Andronicus is Shakespeare's worst play. It is a bad play because Shakespeare tries to \\"shock and awe\\" us with an arbitrary string of horrible things happening. However, what it really does is to bore us. Because it becomes predictable: whenever you come across a likeable character, you know something bad -- or something awful -- will happen to him or her. This syndrome of course was already present in the other books in this series, but in FFC Martin really succumbs. Were you eager to know what happened to the Hound? Well, he's dead. Did you come to like Brienne even if her POVs are infinitely dull? Well, she gets half of her face chewed off and then she is hanged. Was Davos Seaworth one of the nicest guys in Westeros? Well, now you learn he is (problably) dead and had his hands and feet hacked off. And we all know Tyrion lost his nose, Jaime lost his sword hand and Catelyn became a zombie. I'm not saying that bad things should'nt happen to characters I like; I'm just saying that, after a while, it ceases to be \\"bold\\" and \\"realistic\\" and it becomes just another predictable cliche.   * The Robert Jordan syndrome. Robert Jordan could write hundreds of pages without advancing the plot even a little bit; he seemed to think that anything happening to this or that character was worth telling. So he tells us how someone went to some inn, how he or she wandered about the common room, then went outside to breath some fresh air, then got to the common room once again, then sipped some beer, then went outside, then came back to the inn, then went to his or her room and slept, then woke up and... well, you got it. His books got too long and henever finished his series.   *The James Clavell syndrome. Except for Shogun, almost all of James Clavell's books are packed with legions of useless, superficial  and underdeveloped characters, that simply get in and out of the main action without leaving any remarkable mark upon our memories. That's what happens with most character in the ironborn and dorne subplots. I actually liked the action in the ironborn subplot; I just cant understand why it had to be told through three different POVs. The thing gets even worst with the dornish; for me the Hootah and the Oakheart chapters are a mistery. They are simply useless. I couldn't bring myself to care about any of those new POVs characters, although I found the plot itself interesting at times.     All that said, I must add that I did enjoy Jaime's chapters.     Hopefully, all those flaws will be mended in the next volume. I still trust Martin's extraordinary talent as a storyteller. He wont disapoint us in the end, I'm sure.

This CD is a must for every Joe Bonamassa fan, and I would   recommend it to all who enjoy great Blues-Rock.

Adding to those ideas, Dan Roam published his own problem-solving system in a book called The Back of the Napkin.<br /><br />The main idea of the book is the usefulness of drawing simple pictures when analyzing the root cause of a problem, brainstorming solutions, and presenting proposed solutions to senior management. Roam's system is straightforward, creative, and replicable over time. He helps focus problem-solvers on the classic questions associated with any problem: who/what, where, when, why, how, and how many.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed this book and will be incorporating some of the ideas into my own toolkit. 159420229X|4.0|Joshua Foer spent a year of his life, as part of a research assignment, working on his memory skills. By visualizing \\"memory palaces\\" in his mind, he was able to reconstruct some amazing things like the order of a deck of cards, or the names of random people matched to their faces, or strings of digits in a row. The techniques he used allowed him to form mental images that border on the obscene and bizarre, and make total complete sense. Thus is the premise of his fascinating new book (with one slight flaw), \\"Moonwalking with Einstein\\".<br /><br />After watching the U.S. Memory Championships one year, Foer sets off to train himself to participate in the following year contest. He gets support from fellow \\"rememberers\\", both national and international, to improve his chances, and trains daily for the event. Through this journey, Foer explains many of the theories behind how to remember, which in and of themselves is a fascinating read. Foer reaches back into history to detail the progress of memory, when once it was a time-honored tradition and a necessity before the printed word, but now is being ruthlessly attacked by Post-it Notes, cell phones, and other devices that are becoming our digital memory itself.<br /><br />As Foer trains, he describes the things he's visualizing, and it is both raucously comical and disturbing, and dare I say, memorable? The trick he uses to remember a deck of cards (which in turn inspired the title of this tome) intrigues those of us interested in such devices. But as Foer says, all it is is a device, and one that anyone of average intelligence can learn. In fact, people with incredible memories aren't necessarily the brightest, but possibly the most creative.<br /><br />I began to wonder how this skills applies to student learning (me being a teacher). Foer addresses that in a chapter in which an innovative high school teacher in the Bronx uses memory techniques to help his students remember. Also introduced to me is Tony Buzan and [[ASIN:0452273226 The Mind Map Book: How to Use Radiant Thinking to Maximize Your Brain's Untapped Potential]], of which I knew little but have since been launched on a journey to learn more. The Bronx teacher uses it as a tool to note take, study for tests, and as a final project. intriguing!<br /><br />One slight pause that I had with the book occured toward the middle of the end, when Foer goes on the \\"attack\\" (for that's how it read to me) of Daniel Tammet, world known as a \\"savant\\" for remembering things at amazing length. Foer goes to great length to question Tammet's progress, coming to the conclusion that he isn't all that he's claimed to be; that he is simply using memory techniques that he knows. Whether that's true or not isn't the point. It does interrupt the narrative flow of the story, and puts an odd and uncomfortable light on the story. Foer debated whether or not to include it; he should have listened to that little voice inside his memory palace, and left it there untouched and unwritten.<br /><br />However, the book soars in so many other aspects that I have to recommend it. As Foer enters the contest, the climax of the story is compelling. You are rooting for this novice competitor. In the end, a thought began to occur to me, though. Memorizing shuffled decks of cards? Memorizing strings of numbers? What is the point? When  was the last time I had to memorize things of little consequence? Foer comes to a similar conclusion in his epilogue, in which he reflects about the year, and how, despite all of his training, he still loses his keys from time to time.<br /><br />Still, the art of memory is fascinating, and one definitely worth exploring, and you can't begin your exploring in an more entertaining way that starting to Moonwalk with Einstein ... just stay away from Bill Clinton and his basketball!d Map Book: How to Use Radiant Thinking to Maximize Your Brain's Untapped Potential]], of which I knew little but have since been launched on a journey to learn more. 0756404738|5.0|This review may not seem like a five-star review, because I will be pointing out things that irked me as well as things that didn't. However, to believe that a five-star book must be without faults is silly. Relative to other fantasy novels out there, or really just novels in general, this book is easily five stars. I loved it and was sorry to see it end. This review does not contain spoilers per se, but there might be spoilers of omission...if that makes any sense.    That being said, the man (a certain Mr. Rothfuss) is damnable in his pacing, his tangents, and his unwillingness to move us any closer to answering some questions we've had since about ten seconds into \\"The Name of the Wind\\" (spoilers of omission?). All he does is twirl our heartstrings ever-tighter around his greasy fingers.    Ok, I lied. There're going to be spoilers here. This isn't going to be a review for people who haven't yet read the book. That being said, I'll try not to be too explicit.    To say he takes a leisurely stroll in terms of the pace of this story would be to do a disservice to leisurely strolls everywhere. I've seen glaciers move at a faster speed. I have QUESTIONS. And I want ANSWERS. No, I don't want to spend 400 pages enjoying the many fine aspects of the University. No, I don't care about learning a particular brand of sign language for the better part of 300 pages. No, I'm not interested in beautiful faeries. I want to know about the Chandrian. One of my favorite parts of the book is when Kvothe politely skipped over being set upon by pirates. I thought that was rather kind of him.    That is not to say, however, that these parts of the book weren't fascinating. The previous paragraph was about 50% humorous. The man has a gift, there is no denying. He is telling many stories inside of one story, and he is successful. I am just chomping at the bit. I actually did find the first bit, where he is still at the University, to drag somewhat. It smacked too much of \\"The Name of the Wind.\\" Same problems, same cast of characters. However, the saga where he goes to Vintas I found to be particularly fun to read.    That's why I frowned at the \\"sudden\\" turn in events away from that, involving a particular Faerie. That is the first time I've felt like something has just come up out of nowhere in these books. It was a little too...deus ex machina isn't the right word, but it's the word I'm going to use. \\"Oh, there she is. Let's put this very serious and interesting part of the story on hold for a couple hundred pages.\\" This was the low-point in the book for me, both in timing and execution, which is to say it was still very, very good.     I would've found the trip to Ademre much more engaging if I hadn't been wondering when he was going to get back to Vintas and tie up that particular loose end. It was a fascinating point in the story, and I hope the Adem make a reappearance in the third book, but I found myself skimming parts of it to get back to the meat.    At the end of the novel, I felt like nothing of real merit has been accomplished, and we're just back where we started. I can't quite see where the third book is going to go. Which is a good thing, I suppose. It was a \\"ripping good yarn\\" and I barely put it down over the few days it took me to read it, except to go to work (reading it occasionally AT work) and go to sleep (and sometimes forgoing sleep).     Rothfuss is an intriguing author. He eschews regular fantasy diction, or really anything unnecessarily high-handed. \\"Pretentious\\" is the last word I'd use to describe his writing style. His world-building is exceptional, but heavily downplayed in just the right way. Kvothe takes it for granted that people listening to his story, in his world, know certain things (he actually speaks directly to this point once, \\"everyone knows the Caeldim are moneylenders...etc.). Which helps the story to always feel organic and logical (except for a few missteps, such as Felurian), and he manages to constantly keep me on the edge of my seat. Which is really hard to do, considering the glacial pace and the thousand-page length of this novel. I love reading these books, and they only frustrate me in the way great books can--that is, they leave me wanting more.

0756404738|5.0|Several reviews have focused on the oddly-shaped plot, in that our title character, after 1000 pages, is geographically right back where he started, and developmentally not a whole heck of a lot further away than that.<br /><br />This is true but incidental to the question of whether or not this is a good book. Perhaps the expectation is that, by the end of book two in a trilogy, Our Hero ought to have vanquished at least one evil arch-villain, and certainly he ought to have fought in the Battle of Helm's Deep. Instead, our hero learns some languages, hangs out with cultures alien to him, makes (and loses) some friends, and discovers he is both very good and also completely clueless when it comes to the opposite sex. The largest battle in the book -- if you could call it that -- has thirty people, most of whom don't do anything. Wise Man's Fear acquits itself well as a self-contained novel, avoiding the middle child trap: enough is resolved to satisfy the reader while the long-term plot threads are developed just enough to hold our attention for a couple of years until the trilogy is finished.<br /><br />A lot of fantasy authors hit you over the head with their Epic scale. They've put so much research and effort into developing their creation that they want you to 'get it,' and so inundate you with the various languages, cultures, and histories that comprise their universe. Forced narratives deliver a mountain of exposition to show off how detailed their fantasy universe is. And it may well be that Rothfuss' world is not appreciably more epic or more detailed than, say, Sanderson or Tolkien, but Rothfuss knows how saturated the entire genre has become and isn't trying to sell us his story because the plot is any better, or even really any different, than every other fantasy series. It's not the story: it's the storytelling.<br /><br />Rothfuss lets us in on this notion with Kvothe's own Edema Ruh, the nomadic band of troupers in which the main character spends his early years. To paraphrase Kvothe's own explanation, most troupers and bards know the same stories, just as most everyone has heard the best stories and songs before. What makes Wise Man's Fear and Name of the Wind compelling is that they are told so artfully. The characters and the world itself are not narrated in a top-down fashion; rather, we learn about them just as Kvothe does: as a teenager discovering everything for the first time. The 'Chronicler' is writing down Kvothe's story as he tells it, and no doubt has some experience with Amazon.com reviews, as he even complains on a couple occasions that Kvothe is glossing over something that is surely of interest, and Kvothe shrugs it off; he's telling his story his way, and he gets to decide what's important.<br /><br />Rothfuss has been compared to George R. R. Martin, and while both are easily among the finest living fantasy authors around today, they are otherwise nearly incomparable. Martin is weaving a gigantic, elaborate web of relationships and betrayals, and one imagines that the gears started turning over at HBO as soon as the first book of 'Song of Ice and Fire' was finished. Rothfuss is less ambitious and more focused, in part because his story, like so many of Kvothe's own stories, has more in common with an oral tradition than it does with era-spanning gargantuans: it is the proverbial old man by the fireplace to Martin's HBO spectacular. To some, this is a shortcoming, but it's what makes Rothfuss unique. It is probably impossible, or at least inadvisable, to translate The Kingkiller Chronicle to the screen. Its strength is not in a meticulously crafted machine of moving parts, like Martin's Westeros; this story relies entirely upon the sheer force of personality behind both its protagonist, Kvothe, and Rothfuss himself.<br /><br />In other words: this is possibly the only story out of the modern fantasy genre that I would ever want to read aloud. The plot isn't always consistent, and maybe some of the architectural beams creak under the weight of it at times, but one imagines that Rothfuss is as unconcerned with that as he'd like us to be: he's traveling around with his trouper's wagons and he wants us to believe that the delivery matters as much as the content. There's nobody else like Rothfuss out there right now, and even if Kvothe isn't sufficiently 'epic' for some readers, he and many of the other characters have a lot more to say about the simple and confounding business of being human than anything else you'll find in the fantasy section.weight of it at times, but one imagines that Rothfuss is as unconcerned with that as he'd like us to be: he's traveling around with his trouper's wagons and he wants us to believe that the delivery matters as much as the content. There's nobody else like Rothfuss out there right now, and even if Kvothe isn't sufficiently 'epic' for some readers, he and many of the other characters have a lot more to say about the simple and confounding business of being human than anything else you'll find in the fantasy section.

157912688X|5.0|With the obvious exception of Miss Marple, Agatha Christie seldom used female characters as her protagonists, which is a pity since she was perfectly capable of creating a range of vivid and likeable young heroines. In this case, her main female character may also be a murderess...or a victim...it's up to Hercule Poirot to find out.<br /><br />Elinor Carlisle is perfectly content with her lot in life - reasonably well off, young and attractive, and engaged to the love of her life: Roddy Welman. Her only concern is that she not demonstrate *too* much affection for him, as he dislikes excessive devotion, and so she maintains a cool, controlled exterior to hide just how passionately she adores him. Yet there's trouble on the horizon: after receiving an anonymous letter that warns her someone might be after her great-aunt's money, Elinor and Roddy travel down to Hunterbury to visit their invalid relative, Laura Welman. Though they are fond of her, they are also relying on her inheritance to continue their comfortable life after matrimony.<br /><br />Everything seems relatively normal there. Laura has recently suffered from a stroke, but is being cared for by two competent nurses and a young girl who was raised on the property: Mary Gerrard. But Roddy takes one look at the beautiful blonde girl and falls head over heels for her. Dignified but heartbroken, Elinor breaks off the engagement. With her aunt's passing, she finds that without the existence of any will the entirely of the estate passes to her as her only blood relative, and invites Mary Gerrard and Nurse Hopkins to the house in order to help her sort out the household possessions.<br /><br />By the end of the afternoon Mary Gerrard will be dead, and Elinor arrested for her murder. All the evidence points directly at Elinor: she had the opportunity, the motive and the means, and only the desperate pleas of the family doctor brings Hercule Poirot onto the scene to prove her innocence - if indeed she *is* innocent.<br /><br />With the title taken from a mournful passage in Shakespeare's \\"Twelfth Night,\\" the novel is divided into three distinct parts. The first are Elinor's recollections of events up until Mary's death as she stands in the dock, the second is Poirot's investigation, and the third is a courtroom drama as new evidence comes to light, conveyed mostly through dialogue.<br /><br />This is one of Christie's most underrated mysteries. Much like [[ASIN:1579127363 The Hollow]] it is quite character-driven, and due to the presence of what she often called \\"the eternal triangle,\\" it's more emotional than usual. It's hard not to invest yourself in the remote-yet-vulnerable Elinor who is a fascinating blend of passion and coldness, and the mystery that surrounds her: *did* she killed Mary Gerrard?<br /><br />Christie provides a wrap-up with a difference considering the truth comes out in a court of law (instead of Poirot assembling everyone in the library), and a plethora of intriguing clues that can be as minute as an off-handed comment about roses. It is perhaps a little slow-paced, but the tension builds gradually right to the denouncement, making it one of my favorite Christie mysteries.aken from a mournful passage in Shakespeare's \\"Twelfth Night,\\" the novel is divided into three distinct parts. The first are Elinor's recollections of events up until Mary's death as she stands in the dock, the second is Poirot's investigation, and the third is a courtroom drama as new evidence comes to light, conveyed mostly through dialogue.     This is one of Christie's most underrated mysteries. Much like [[ASIN:1579127363 The Hollow]] it is quite character-driven, and due to the presence of what she often called \\"the eternal triangle,\\" it's more emotional than usual. It's hard not to invest yourself in the remote-yet-vulnerable Elinor who is a fascinating blend of passion and coldness, and the mystery that surrounds her: *did* she killed Mary Gerrard?     Christie provides a wrap-up with a difference considering the truth comes out in a court of law (instead of Poirot assembling everyone in the library), and a plethora of intriguing clues that can be as minute as an off-handed comment about roses. It is perhaps a little slow-paced, but the tension builds gradually right to the denouncement, making it one of my favorite Christie mysteries.

B0034G4ORC|4.0|With Battle: Los Angeles I expected a lot of CG effects and little plot. It was refreshing to find that the film concentrated more on the characters than glitzy effects.  This is a war movie. When Earth is invaded by aliens there are no questions about have they been watching us or what they want. The marines at the center of this drama are only concerned about their objective: rescuing civilians caught in the middle of the battle.      Battle: Los Angeles has at its core a standard story of redemption. Sgt. Michael Nantz (Aaron Eckhart) led his squad on an apparent suicide mission where he was the only survivor.  He is shunned by fellow Marines as a pariah and, in the opening scene, submits a request for retirement.  When the alien attacks begin, Nantz is assigned to serve under a newly commissioned Second Lieutenant William Martinez (Ramon Rodriguez). The other members of  the platoon resent Nantz and think he would get them all killed.  This is particularly true for Corporal Jason Lockett (Cory Hardrict) whose brother was killed in Nantz's prior mission.      As is standard for this kind of film, we get a brief glimpse into the lives of the characters. We discover that Lt. Martinez's wife is expecting a baby; another soldier is planning his wedding.  There is just enough information to provide a little insight.  The approach of the filmmakers was to take the point of view of a squad of soldiers.  Like them, we are unaware of what kinds of creatures have invaded Earth, what their weapons are and how the battle can be won.  Battle: Los Angeles could have been very mediocre but for the excellent performance of Aaron Eckhart. He perfectly conveys the guilt he carries for being a survivor and the ends he is willing to go through to prove himself to the squad and his lieutenant. Michael Pena plays a civilian who is rescued by the squad along with his young son, nicely played by Bryce Cass.  Brigit Moynahan is good in a small role as a vet who discovers how to kill thealiens.     In all, Battle: Los Angeles will probably remind you of other war films but it has enough suspense and good performances to keep you on the edge of your seat. The CG effects are good and are not used for mindless dazzle.

0321344758|5.0|As designers we are constantly struggling to please our bosses, who often don't agree with the way we design things and want it done their way, which, as any designer can tell you, is never a good way. There is a reason behind our madness. Everything else is just clutter on a page. Steve Krug's book \\"Don't Make Me Think\\" helped me put my frustrations into words that others could understand, so that when I explain, \\"Element A clashes with element B,\\" or \\"This design needs to be simplified because of X, Y, and Z,\\" I'm not met with a bunch of blank stares.<br /><br />This book is excellent in every way - well written, beautifully designed, visually interesting, fun to read, and/or - if you don't have a lot of time - easy to skim through just to pick up a few pointers (though it's well worth a good read... or two.) The author writes to the experienced designer and also to the layman who's just starting out. If you're new to web design, you'll learn TONS; if you're a seasoned pro, you'll get a solid refresher and maybe even pick up on a few new things.<br /><br />And although this book is geared toward web design, there is so much in it that is applicable to graphic design in general. The title alone is a resounding example: Don't make me think. If your designs - whether for internet marketing, billboards, magazines, or whatever - require people to look twice, or think too hard, you risk losing them.

I will continue to wait for that all encompassing CD from Ms. Hudson but in the meantime, I got this....

B004J4WQ46|4.0|Angles is a really GOOD album. But it really stops there. To me the struggles of this album even coming together stemmed from the negative reactions their last album, First Impressions of Earth, received. To me The Strokes first 3 albums were 100% 5-star albums, each perfect and unique in its own way. I realize how different FIOE came across to even the most die-hard Strokes fan but I truly admire that album for it's true confidence. It was a milestone from a band unafraid to dip a toe in unfamiliar territory and that only added to the excitement and mystery for us fans to wonder, \\"Whatever will they try next?!\\" FIOE was like a statement for their fans as if to say, \\"This is what we're into writing right now. If you dig it, GREAT! If not, it's all good.\\" The Strokes had faith in their fans and unfortunately many abandoned them. But regardless of how negative \\"fans\\" were towards FIOE it should never have stopped them from going in whatever direction they wanted to.    This is where my issues with Angles arise. To me, this album beginning to end sounds like The Strokes trying to sound like The Strokes rather than play and create what feels natural. Even the interior photography strongly resembles a glossier version of the Is This It? photos. It's like they want us to be reminded that they're still \\"Jenny from the block.\\" The problem is for those of us that love them simply because they write great songs and not just because their \\"cool\\" people, we don't really need to fall in love with them again because we've never stopped. We don't need a \\"return to form\\" because we like where they were headed and would follow them no matter what! I seem to be in the minority that doesn't LOVE \\"Under Cover of Darkness\\" and my reasoning is that this song out of all 10 on Angles sounds the most of all overly worked it's not even funny. I mean I understand being meticulous but there comes a point where you just gotta back away from a song and leave it be. \\"Under Cover of Darkness\\" is like a compromising backwards step for them and a message to those unnerved \\"fans\\" as if they're saying \\"Look we can still write a song like \\"Last Nite!\\" Whereas past singles i.e. \\"Last Nite\\" \\"Reptilia\\" and \\"Juicebox\\" just rolled with the punches with brilliant fluidity, there's just TOO dang much going on with this new single. It's not a BAD song but it's something we've heard them do much better in the past. Almost every song on this album sounds like things couldn't be entirely agreed on or something. The standouts for me on this album are \\"Taken for A Fool\\" \\"You're so Right\\" \\"Games\\" and \\"Life is Simple in the Moonlight.\\" To me these songs BELONG on the same album and display The Strokes we love but with some very interesting new ideas. It's them once again unafraid to grow as a band. YES there is an obvious Radiohead influence on \\"You're so Right\\" and I would even argue I hear a little \\"Satellite\\" by Guster going on with \\"Life is Simple in the Moonlight\\" to an extent. I'm not arguing for what's ALL 100% uninfluenced and what's not because let's face it, EVERYONE is influenced by something and no one, NOT EVEN RADIOHEAD is 100% original. But these songs to me are the most fluid sounding of the album. A lot of everything else, particularly \\"Gratisfaction\\" and \\"Metabolism\\" hint at some great ideas but never quite get there. A lot of it just sounds incomplete. The tools are there but some songs don't seem to be as melodically built as well as in past Strokes albums. Now people keep saying \\"this is it for the Strokes.\\" I mean I think that's a bit of a stretch. As I've said this is still a really good album. But I just hope The Strokes will get back to making albums for themselves and trust that fans will stick by them no matter what. I know a lot of people jumped ship after FIOE but even though Angles isn't my favorite out of the four, I will be a Strokes fan till the day I die. For me they have nothing to prove anymore and can do anything they want. But that's exactly what I want them to do is to write songs for THEMSELVES and whatever people say about it is just what they say. They don't need to try to win people over because there's always going to be people who just can't be swayed back no matter what. Again, Angles is really good and if you even LIKE The Strokes there are some definite cool, fluid, catchy in a good way moments here. I would love to say this album is a masterpiece and their best ever, they've outdone themselves yadda yadda yadda. But I want to be fair and honest. And it seems even the members themselves know this isn't the best thing they've ever done. Like I said, The Strokes don't have a \\"bad\\" song in them, regardless of what you haters say about \\"15 Minutes\\" haha. But next time around I just want The Strokes to be themselves and the truest fans will always stick by them no matter what.
1594202842|2.0|In all the hubbub about this book, some sad episodes have been overlooked.  The most heartbreaking, worse than the way Chua berates and reviles her children, was when her mother-in-law, a vibrant, accomplished woman, \\"begged for\\" one full day with each grandchild.  Chua didn't even consider it, as it would have required time away from the demanding regimen of winning prizes and impressing people.  That loving grandmother is now dead, but Chua doesn't reflect on the choice she made: valuing prizes and accolades over her children's relationship with their grandmother.  Elsewhere Chua congratulates herself on how wonderful she was to take in this grandmother when she was ill with leukemia.  Chua's husband indicated his worry about how the two women would coexist, how his mother would cope with the screaming rages Chua aimed at her daughters.  But Chua volunteered to take grandma in anyway.  \\"It's the Chinese way\\", she said, since in her opinion \\"Western parents get thrown into nursing homes when they're old\\" (an example of how little Chua knows about average Americans.)  But wait!  What's this?  Did grandma come to stay?  Why no!  She stayed with the Chua family for a mere month, then was shuttled back to her apartment, where she died alone.  This is the Chinese way that Chua can't stop bragging about?    Another telling but neglected incident:  Chua ran over her daughter's foot.  She blamed the child for getting out of the car too quickly while she was backing up.  Picture it.  Was hard charging Chua to blame, too self-absorbed to step on the brake?   No, no, it was Sophia's fault!  On the bright side, \\"at least it gave her a lot of time to practice the piano.\\"    The children had pet rabbits at one point.  Chua hated the rabbits as they were \\"unintelligent\\".  She claims she would have nothing to do with them.  But the rabbits \\"mysteriously escaped\\".   Chua as Glenn Close.    Chua credits her children's accomplishments to their being Chinese.  Their Jewish American father, a man of normal American upbringing, bright and accomplished, gets no credit.  But let's examine Chinese accomplishments vs. Jewish ones, using Chua's favorite proof of superiority: prizes.  And let's look at real prizes, not the gold stickered school certificates that Chua goes gaga over.  Jews won 163 Nobel prizes in the last hundred years, despite being a tiny minority (13 million today).  The billion Chinese people that Chua reveres have won a total of ten Nobel prizes, if you include Americans of Chinese ancestry.  Chua cites a few obscure studies to prove the greatness of Chinese methods of child raising, but you'd think she'd have noticed that the prizes of childhood don't prove anything except that some children have rabid stage mothers.      Chua's ideas are the only ones that count.  She dictates every move her children make, puts herself at the center of every incident, and worships herself.  She says, \\"My last name is Chua...and I love it.\\"    She informs her daughter that following rules and winning prizes is all that matters, then says, \\"I was deeply moved by my oration.\\"  She describes the activity of her months old baby as \\"watching me have writer's block.\\"  Her own daughter told her, \\"You're a showoff.  It's all about you.\\"  Though her husband attempts to serve as buffer between her and the children, in the book he never shows the backbone required to win an argument with this termagant.      Born in the U.S., Amy Chua considers herself a \\"Chinese mother\\".  An American who showed such prejudice and disdain for another country as Chua shows for the U.S. would be called an Ugly American.  Chua regards the United States with contempt; her favorite insult is calling something \\"typical American\\".  (And unfortunately, by the end of the book, her daughters are talking like this too.)  Chua doesn't seem well acclimated to this country, and sometimes comes off as a foreigner, such as when she describes what she considers inane sports encouragement, claiming to quote parents as saying \\"Go team spirit\\".  (!)  She demands that her daughters make toasts at a party for her husband's 50th birthday, oblivious to the vulgarity of children delivering toasts, even though one daughter had the good taste to be repelled by the idea and refuse.    How did this deluded woman become a Yale professor?  Chua says she interviewed for a job at Yale, where her husband already taught.  She didn't do well and wasn't called back.  Some time later she was suddenly and mysteriously offered a tenured position at Yale.  She claims this was due to one paper she wrote.  I suspect there's more to this story...    How does a working mother have the time to hover over her children for most of the day?  She admits to skipping out during her office hours to drive the children around to their many appointments, and one has to wonder why there is no consequence for her casual attitude toward work.  She admits to taking her girls out of school for music lessons, driving them for many hours hither and yon, returning them to school after.  At home she dominates marathon practices, schoolwork and tutors.  The lax work schedule of a tenured professor in the Ivy League is obviously something to envy, though it also makes a reader question her high opinion of the Chinese work ethic.  Or perhaps that ethic only applies to children.    Chua reveals herself to be a racist and a snob.  Her disdain toward anyone not of her ilk was skin crawling.  She scoffs at janitors, calls people who can't master difficult music \\"losers\\", admits to favoring her law students \\"with strict Asian parents\\".  She's very fond of the word \\"prestigious\\".  She makes the ridiculous claim \\"there are all these new books out there portraying Asian mothers as scheming, callous, overdriven people...\\"   She doesn't name any, and a reader will be hard pressed to think of one.  \\"Ethnicity was my favorite thing to talk about\\" is shown to be true by her endless \\"Chinese good/Western bad\\" prattling.    When her husband used his law degree in the public sector as a federal prosecutor in New York City, Chua's parents were appalled.  Chua has the same rigid judgments, and maps out the only acceptable path for her children -- God forbid her child should want to join the armed forces or work in government.  Chua's Chinese values are self-absorbed, scorning public service and aiming only for self-glorification and elitist prestige.  According to Chua's description of Chinese values, serving one's country isn't admirable, it's a crime.    If this was a book about stressing academic achievement, cutting out the screen time, and encouraging focused discipline in outside pursuits, it would be true.  But no one would read it.  It's Chua's screaming, raging torment of her children, her selfish inability to see any viewpoint but her own, her racist ranting about her Chinese superiority, and her ridicule of the U.S. that draw readers like rubbernecking voyeurs at a grisly accident.  Now that there's been significant blowback from horrified readers, Chua has beaten a retreat.  She's gone on TV and radio to backtrack, claiming she really didn't mean what she said, but she's already betrayed her true self.  She lacks self awareness and insight; she's a lockstep follower of a rigid plan, not even a plan of her own devising but one she inherited.  For all her vaunted education, she is incapable of examining her assumptions and working out a new path, even when her chosen path led to a self-mutilating child.  Chua's mother-in-law died without the longed for day with her granddaughters that she begged for; Chua's daughters lost forever the opportunity to have one memorable day with their grandmother.  To Chua, gloating as she gazes at her daughters' sparkly awards, she made the right Chinese decision.  Those are her Chinese values.

You must go online via browser and sign in to your Amazon account, go to the \\"Appstore for Android\\" page, click on \\"Your Apps & Devices\\" right under the search box, then DE-REGISTER YOUR DEVICE (tiny-print link on the right side).  But once you do that, you can't open Angry Birds Rio -- you are prompted to log in: \\"Please Sign In. You must be signed in to the Amazon App Store to run this app. Login / Quit.\\"    It follows that you must be logged in to Amazon Appstore forever in order to use any of the apps you acquire through Amazon Appstore.  This also means you can never uninstall Amazon Appstore, because if you don't have the app, you can't log in.

B004HKE96K|5.0|It is a monumental disservice to paint the Drive-By Truckers and Southern Rock Opera into a corner by claiming that either is 'Southern Rock'.     Is the band from and the album about 'The South'? Sure. Was it a great way to sell the album? Absolutely. But, there are no Confederate flags flying over these proceedings. There are no guest appearances by Charlie Daniels or Richard Betts.     The 'opera' part - the Lynyrd Skynyrd references (particularly noted by reviewers) - those may draw a listener to the album. But the power of the album comes from songs that reveal a time and place that has many realities. A place where, as the lyrics go 'a whole lot of good people dragged threw the blood and glass'.    I read one review on Amazon that claimed that perhaps it was only necessary to listen to the track 'The Three Great Alabama Icons' once. How incredibly wrong that would be. Listen to it to remind yourself what the album is about. 'Southern Rock Opera' is about the South. It's about rock n' roll. It's about being a kid. It's about the duality of the southern thing.    Southern Rock Opera is a cultural document worth spending many hours digesting. And god damn, it's a friggin' great rock n' roll album.

B00475AYJQ|2.0|If this was the First book in a series, nobody would read the second.  Everything about the meandering storyline/character/plot/etc has been said, so I will just say this.      This writing is witty, but only witty  in a way that makes absolutely no sense when applied to a fantasy setting.  Rothfuss's main source of wit comes from plays on words that only apply to the English language.  When applied to a fantasy setting, these same witticisms make no sense at all, unless of course, the fantasy world somehow employs English as its primary language.  This flaw happens multiple times throughout both books, but due to the Second book's drastic downturn in quality, I decided to bring it up.      <<<A HOMOPHONE is a word that is pronounced the same as another word but differs in meaning. The words may be spelled the same, such as rose (flower) and rose (past tense of \\"rise\\"), or differently, such as carat, caret, and carrot, or two and too.>>>     Patrick is using plays on English words, as well as rhymes based on English pronunciations to accomplish most of his 'wit'.  If he was writing a story in a real world English speaking setting, what he said would make sense, but not come across as particularly clever.  Used in the Kingkiller setting, it makes no sense at all.     This happens so many times throughout Rothfuss's books that is isn't even worth cataloging in this post.  If you know what I am referring to, or are interested in finding it yourself, just look through the book again.  Here is a hint: Paraphrased from the text,   \\"Lackless, one of the versions of this surname, was the basis for the word \\"Lackey\\".
